    "primarily on deterring high-end cyber threats emanating from major and ‘lesser' powers and sophisticated non-state actors. The DSB makes several important suggestions. First, it introduces the term ‘deterrence by cost imposition' to replace the more commonly used term deterrence by punishment. The rationale, it would seem, is to broaden punishment's scope in keeping with the emerging logic of cross-domain deterrence for cyberspace. ‘Cost imposition' better captures the kinetic, diplomatic, economic, law enforcement, and digital forms of punishment that the US might use in responding to cyberattacks and exploitations. Second, the DSB bridges the divide between denial and punishment in cyberspace. ‘DoD must create a second-strike cyber resilient “Thin Line”' of critical US offensive systems (cyber, nuclear and conventional) and related infrastructure in order to ensure that the US can credibly threaten retaliation in response to a potentially crippling cyber first strike. Good defence and resilience feeds into the potency and certainty of threats of punishment. Third, the DSB affirms, rather bluntly, the continued asymmetry between offense and defence in cyberspace. ‘The unfortunate reality', the 2017 DSB report reads, ‘is that, for at least the coming five to ten years, the offensive cyber capabilities of our most capable potential adversaries are likely to far exceed the United Sates' ability to defend and adequately strengthen the resilience of its critical infrastructures'. US cyber offense, then, is needed to correct the perceptions of asymmetry between defenders and challengers. Finally, the DSB links cyber deterrence to reputation, past and future. It warns that not responding to a cyberattack -- once, presumably, US cyber deterrence has failed -- is, with ‘near-certainty ... an invitation to follow-on cyber attacks'. In sum, the DSB Task Force solidifies the primary role offense will have in US cyber deterrence posture and strategy of the emerging decades.\n\nWith its offensive building blocks in place, the US, first under President Obama and later, under President Trump, began flexing its cyber muscle. It surreptitiously destroyed centrifuges in Iran, sabotaging and delaying the country's nuclear weapons program. It explored using cyberspace to dismantle Libya's air defences leading up to the March 2011 military intervention. The US likewise targeted and sabotaged North Korea's missile development program. Elsewhere, Cybercom was charged with attacking ISIS's online presence and capability in support of US and coalition plans to degrade and defeat the terrorist organisation, and to digitally augment related kinetic operations in Syria, Iraq, and elsewhere. The US is thought to have also used cyberspace to retaliate against North Korea following its 2014 hack on Sony Pictures. Other forms of punishment were meted out by",
    "#\n\nA. S. WILNER\n\nthe Justice Department. New cyber attribution capabilities allowed DOJ, in 2014, 2016, and 2018 to indict over a dozen Chinese military hackers, seven Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps personnel, and another two private Iranian citizens for stealing US intellectual property, computer hacking, and launching ransomware attacks, and several Russian military intelligence officers for a string of hacks targeting the Netherlands-based Organization for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons, and sporting and anti-doping agencies in the US, Canada and Europe. Via EO 13687, President Obama likewise imposed additional sanctions on North Korea in January 2015 in direct response to the Sony Hack.[84] Finally, in his last days in office, President Obama sanctioned and expelled Russian nationals for their role in hacking the 2016 presidential election. Another 13 Russian nationals and three companies were subsequently charged by the Justice Department in February 2018, as part of Special Council Robert S. Mueller III's investigation into Russian's meddling in the 2016 US Presidential Election. Russia was again fingered by American and British officials in April 2018 for targeting and exploiting network infrastructure devices, like routers, for purposes of espionage.[85] The coercive message, all told, is that the US has developed the capability to trace and attribute cyberattacks conducted against it and its allies, and to launch punitive measures in cyberspace and across domains in response.\n\nIn hand with this shift towards cyber punishment, Obama also developed US declaratory posture for cyberspace and helped establish international norms for cyber engagement. From a deterrence perspective, it is not enough to simply have the tools to harm an adversary: you also need to establish and communicate red lines against which acts of retaliation will be measured. Norms that help identify what is and is not acceptable behaviour in cyberspace further solidify retaliatory threats. Obama's 2011 International Strategy for Cyberspace is the backbone of this process. 'The digital world is no longer a lawless frontier', President Obama's opening statement to the document reads, 'it is a place where the norms of responsible, just, and peaceful conduct among states and people have begun to take hold'.[86] The strategy outlines how the US would provide leadership in establishing global norms for cyber behaviour. The US State Department, for instance, went on to establish the world's first Office of the Coordinator for Cyber Issues (S/CCI) in February 2011, dedicated to coordinating the Department's diplomatic work on cyber and developing international cyber rules.     "THE JOURNAL OF STRATEGIC STUDIES\n265\n\nother states later inaugurated their own, similar, offices. S/CCI’s greatest success was the 2015 US-China agreement on economic cyber espionage – a bilateral arrangement that China subsequently agreed to sign with the UK, Canada, Australia, the G-7, and G-20 – that codified that neither party would conduct ‘cyber-enabled theft of intellectual property’.87 The hope is that these bilateral and multilateral initiatives will curtail particular forms of behaviour in cyberspace that will eventually inform and help establish more comprehensive norms of engagement.\n\nThe Office of the Coordinator for Cyber Issues was also instrumental in assisting a UN Group of Government Experts (GGE) establish a rudimentary list of cyber norms in 2015 (though the GGE failed in 2017 to subsequently link those norms to recommendations embedded in international law).88 In terms of cyber deterrence, the 2011 Strategy illustrates that the US will continue to bolster denial – redubbed here as ‘dissuasion’ – to deter attacks, but also strongly signals its ‘right to self-defense’ in conducting cyber retaliation. The US, the document reads, ‘will respond to hostile acts in cyberspace as we would to any threat to our country … and we recognize that certain hostile acts … could compel actions under the commitments we have with our military treaty partners. We reserve the right to use all necessary means – diplomatic, informational, military, and economic’.89 In line with these strategic pronouncements, the Obama Administration tried to make a distinction between cyber espionage for the purposes of statecraft – which was deemed legitimate – and cyber espionage, theft, and attack for the purposes of economic or domestic disruption and infrastructure destruction – all deemed illegitimate. Thus, on the one hand, China’s 2015 exfiltration of over 20 million personnel records from the US Office of Personnel Management (OPM) was deemed ‘not an attack’ by James Clapper, director of US National Intelligence, but rather good espionage; ‘[we] would have done the same thing’.90 But on the other hand, Russia’s 2015/16 hack, manipulation, and leak of Democratic Party and Democratic National Committee data during the 2016 presidential election was deemed by CIA Director, John Brennan, as a ‘new level of malicious activity’ meant to undermine and influence the institution of US democracy.91 In January 2017,\n\n87 Adam Segal, ‘The Top Five Cyber Policy Developments of 2015’, Council on Foreign Relations blog, 4 January 2016.\n\n88 UN General Assembly, ‘Group of Governmental Experts on Developments in the Field of Information and Telecommunications in the Context of International Security’, Seventieth Session, Item 93, 2015.\n\n89 The White House, International Strategy for Cyberspace: Prosperity, Security, and Openness in a Networked World, May 2011, 13–14.\n\n90 The 2017 DSB report does however argue, rather cryptically, that ‘some would view’ the OPM heist as an example of ‘egregious behavior’ that crossed the ‘unwritten rules of traditional espionage’, DSB 2017, 7, 11–12. WILNER\n\ntwo weeks before Trump's inauguration, DHS took the unprecedented step of classifying 'election infrastructure' as a critical infrastructure subsector, providing it with priority protection within the NIPP.[92]\n\nWhile the Trump Administration's cyberspace and cyber deterrence strategies are still being patched together, given the evolution of US cyber deterrence to date, and given what we know about Trump's leanings in international affairs, three things are likely to occur under his watch.\n\nFirst, threats of cyber retaliation and the logic of deterrence by punishment will continue to grow in importance. Pentagon officials have long sought more leeway from congress to engage in cyber offense. In 2017, Admiral Rogers, Cybercom's chief, lamented that 'offensive cyber is almost treated like nuclear weapons, in the sense that their application ... is controlled at the chief executive level and not delegated down. What I would like to see, over the next five to 10 years', is that US cyber weapons are viewed 'as another tool for the commanders' at the tactical level.[93] Signaling his acceptance of Rogers' position, President Trump, in August 2017, initiated the elevation of Cybercom to the status of a Unified Combatant Command (a step long-debated, but never taken, under Obama).[94] The transition places Cybercom at par with other unified commands, like US Strategic Command or US European Command, establishing a direct line of communication between Cybercom's director and the Secretary of Defense. And a year later, in August 2018, President Trump rescinded a 2012 Obama-era directive - Presidential Policy Directive 20 - that governed the manner in which cyberattacks were approved.[95] The reversal loosens the (somewhat onerous) restrictions that Obama had placed on conducting offensive cyber operations.\n\nFurthermore, Trump's February 2018 Nuclear Posture Review (the first tabled since 2010), goes to some length in closing the cross-domain gap between nuclear weapons, nuclear retaliation, and cyber deterrence. Just as DoD's aforementioned Defense Science Board report recommended in 2013, the 2018 NPR expands the scope conditions that might lead to a US nuclear response as a result of a cyberattack. While US nuclear retaliation will continue to be reserved for 'extreme circumstances', the 2018 NPR significantly broadens these circumstances themselves to include 'significant non-nuclear strategic attacks', on 'civilian population or infrastructure'. Additionally, perhaps cognizant that technology risks advancing more quickly than strategic policy, the NPR further warns that ‘the United States reserves the right to make any adjustment in the assurance that may be warranted by the evolution and proliferation of non-nuclear strategic attack technologies and U.S. capabilities to counter that threat'. Lest anyone misinterpret the document, DoD simultaneously published the NPR's Executive Summary in several languages, including Japanese, Korean, Chinese and Russian.\n\nThe logic of all of this was rolled into the State Department's ‘Recommendations to the President on Deterring Adversaries and Better Protecting the American People from Cyber Threats', a three-page brief published in May 2018 in direct response to EO 13800, and DoD's new Cyber Strategy, published in September 2018. The ‘Recommendations' report suggests that existing efforts at deterrence in cyberspace are ‘necessary but not sufficient': ‘network defense alone' is not enough. Calling for a ‘fundamental rethinking' of existing frameworks and a ‘new US vision' for cyber deterrence, the report recommends the development of a ‘broader menu of consequences' that the US can impose against state and non-state actors below ‘the threshold of the use of force'. With reference to the perennial issues of attribution and coercive credibility, the document calls for the US to openly demonstrate its ability to identify threats by imposing ‘swift, costly, and transparent consequences' on adversaries. Action will stand for credibility and might have ‘downstream deterrence effects'. Cyber offense is likewise paramount in DoD's 2018 Cyber Strategy. While the strategy itself is not publicity available, a 7-page summary is. And while this new strategy supersedes DoD's 2015 Strategy it compliments DoD's 2018 National Defense Strategy and the 2017 National Security Strategy. The document warns specific adversaries that the US will ‘build a more lethal force', conduct pre-emptive operations, ‘prepare for war', and collect cyber intelligence so as to field the ‘military cyber capabilities' the US needs to ‘defend forward'. A fuzzy term, defending forward implies terminating attacks at their (foreign) source, and ‘leveraging [US] focus outward to stop threats before they reach their targets'. By persistently engaging cyber competitors and aggressors on a daily basis, and by employing the Pentagon's full range of offensive capabilities, the US, the Strategy submits, will deter malicious cyber activities over time.\n\nSecond, and somewhat relatedly, under Trump, non-state actors may be encouraged to conduct cyber punishment and cyber offense themselves. The idea is to permit victims of cyber aggression and crime -- companies,",
    "A. S. WILNER\n\ninstitutions, NGOs, and individuals – to ‘hack back’ or counterattack via ‘active defense’ against their perpetrators. Under the current US Computer Fraud and Abuse Act, it is illegal for individuals to gain entry into another’s computer system without permission. Though several US tech firms, including Google and Microsoft, have voiced concerns, Congress has nonetheless explored exceptions as part of the Active Cyber Defense Certainty Act (first proposed in March 2017), and Georgia’s General Assembly passed a bill (later vetoed by the Governor) that would have made provisions for non-state active defence in cyberspace.⁹⁹ Despite the controversy, some companies, including major US banks, have been reported to have surveyed the utility of hack backs to protect their servers, data, and assets.¹⁰⁰ In a telling episode, Bossert, the former White House security advisor, and Assistant Secretary Jeanette Manfra, the chief cybersecurity official at DHS, heaped rare praise on Facebook and Microsoft during their lengthy December 2017 press-briefing in which the US officially blamed North Korea for conducting the May 2017 WannaCry ransomware attacks. The attacks encrypted data on hundreds of thousands of public and private sector computers in over 150 countries. ‘We applaud our corporate partners’, Bossert asserted, ‘for acting on their own initiative … without any direction by the U.S. government … to disrupt the activities of North Korean hackers. They shut down accounts … used to launch attacks and patched systems’.¹⁰¹ The destructive power and frequency of cyber hacking only grew under Obama. This trend will continue under Trump. Emerging technologies like Artificial Intelligence and wirelessly tethered Internet of Things (IoT) devices, along with new types of malicious activity like ransomware and sophisticated Distributed Denial of Service (DDoS) attacks, both of which can now be bought and sold on the Dark Web, will reshape the cyber threat landscape for individuals, communities, NGOs, and public and private-sector actors.¹⁰² Under the right conditions, non-state hack backs and other disruptive actions – the twenty-first century’s version of digital vigilantism – may prove useful for establishing a whole-of-society – rather than a whole-of-government – US cyber deterrence doctrine.\n\nFinally, in keeping with Trump’s general weariness of multilateralism and the United Nations in particular, the US is likely to downplay its role in establishing global cyber norms and focus instead on communicating its own red lines and bolstering the cyber deterrent posture of like-mined allied\n\n⁹⁹Zaid Shoorbajee, ‘Google and Microsoft ask Georgia Governor to Veto “Hack Back” Bill’, *CyberScoop*, 27 April 2018; Ryan Johnston, ‘After Widespread outcry, Georgia Governor Vetoes “Hack Back” Bill’, *State Scoop*, 8 May 2018.\n¹⁰⁰Dan Lohrmann, ‘Hack Back Law’, *Government Technology*, September 2017; Nicholas Schmidle, ‘The Digital Vigilantes who Hack Back’, *New Yorker*, 7 May 2018.\n¹⁰¹The White House, ‘Press Briefing on the Attribution of the WannaCry Malware Attack to North Korea’, 19 December 2017.\n¹⁰²Bruce Schneier, ‘Botnets of Things’, *MIT Technology Review*, March/April 2017.",

The 2018 'Recommendations' report, for illustration, calls on the US to first establish criteria for US retaliation and to then communicate the policy both 'publicly and privately' so as to generate a deterrent effect. Separately, Trump shuttered the State Department's cyber coordinator's office, effectively degrading America's role in UN negotiations, though the Administration did test the waters for restarting the process of norm building in October 2018, following a ministerial meeting with US allies.[103] What appears more certain, however, is that the US is likely to seek bilateral cyber relations with other states and groups of states. At the Cyber Week 2017 gathering in Israel in June, for instance, the ever-present Bossert announced the US-Israel bilateral cyber working group – which was to be led by White House cyber czar, Rob Joyce, before his departure and the elimination his position – signalling Washington's preference for establishing cyber coalitions of the willing. '[W]e must', Bossert warned, 'hold those who violate [cyber] norms accountable. This may not be achievable through a UN effort. Likeminded states should work to develop options for imposing consequences within a coalition structure'.[104] In a similar vein, the US, in partnership with the UK, Germany and other NATO allies, aims to establish cyber warfare principles for 2019.[105] And, as the White House articulates in its 2018 Cyber Strategy, the US has plans to build a 'Cyber Deterrence Initiative' that will convene a coalition of 'like-minded states' that can collectively develop 'tailored strategies' and jointly impose 'consequences against malign actors'.[106]\n\n# Conclusion: Evolution in theory\n\nUS cyber deterrence has come a long way since 2003. Definitions of deterrence – and core concepts linked to punishment and denial – have been revisited, reconceptualised, and broadened in scope. New government positions, institutions, agencies, and organisations have been created, staffed, trained, and tasked with operationalising core concepts. New technology, including software and hardware, has been developed and deployed. Partnerships across the US government, the private sector, and civil society have been established. And allies and international organisations have been leveraged. The footprints of all of these changes are evident in the record: US strategic doctrine allows us to trace the direction of this evolution. From a theoretical perspective, the way in which US cyber deterrence has been adapted since its inception provides\n\n103Sean Lyngaas, 'US Looks to Restart Talks on Global Cyber Norms', CyberScoop, October 2018.\n\n104The White House, 'Remarks by Homeland Security Advisor Thomas P. Bossert at Cyber Week 2017', 26 June 2017.\n\n105Robin Emmott, 'NATO mulls \"Offensive Defense\" with Cyber Warfare Rules', Reuters, 30 November 2017.\n\n106 National Cyber Strategy of the United States of America, September 2018, 21.",
    "scholars with insight as to how deterrence theory itself might respond to the promises and paradoxes of cyber deterrence. Several lessons stand out.\n\nFirst, US cyber deterrence has shifted from an almost exclusive focus on defence and deterrence by denial under President Bush to a punishment-based strategy under Presidents Obama and Trump. Part of the reason threats of retaliation and ‘deterrence by cost imposition' are currently emphasised is that recent technological advancements have allowed the US to improve its ability to monitor cyberattacks, identify perpetrators, and administer retaliatory actions, both within and outside cyberspace. Simply put, punishment strategies are increasingly feasible. Technological advancements have led to the adoption and use of new offensive coercive strategies. If so, theorists themselves should better situate technology into their own work of deterrence. How might the prerequisites of deterrence theory, and the concepts derived from them, adapt to novel, emerging, and disruptive technology within and beyond cyberspace?\n\nSecond, and relatedly, a separate reason US cyber punishment strategies have come to overshadow denial strategies has to do with the perception that deterrence by denial is not working, or rather, that the threat of denial used alone and separately from the threat of punishment, is not working. The number, severity and complexity of cyberattacks targeting US government and private sector facilities and assets has continued to rise over the past 15 years despite the US government's communicated threat of defence and denial. In response, US doctrine and strategy has evolved such that punishment is being used hand-in-glove with denial. For illustration, synchronising their efforts to defend the 2018 US midterm elections, DoD-DHS collaboration saw the former (via Cyber Command) target Russian operatives overseas to disrupt their actions all the while the latter worked with social media companies to counter disinformation. In practice, the two approaches reinforce each other. Recalibrating the effect punishment and denial have on each other in practice, suggests that theories of deterrence must better relate how the logic of punishment augments, or otherwise corresponds to, the logic of denial. Too often punishment and denial are considered poles apart, distinct levers applied separately to the cost-benefit calculus that underpins rational behaviour and coercion. In theory, how do punishment and denial relate and overlap; in cyberspace when does one stop and the other start?\n\nThird, the US has come to equate attribution as a distinct form of punishment that appears logically linked to denial. In fielding a question during the 2017 WannaCry press briefing, Bossert suggested, for instance, that ‘it's not about holding a country accountable; it's about simple culpability. We've determined who was behind the [WannaCry] attack, and we're saying it. ...",
    "We're going to hold them accountable ... And we're going to shame them for it. ... And I think it's going to improve our security'. By identifying North Korea, the US denied it the benefit of anonymity in cyberspace; by publically outing North Korea, the US put the regime on notice for future punishment. From a theoretical perspective, however, attribution is largely understood as a mechanism, a crucial step that allows a defender to retaliate by identifying a challenger, rather than as a functional punishment in its own right. If so, and in light of perceptual changes in practice, deterrence theory must better explore whether, how, and under what conditions public attribution -- and the threat of shaming -- function as a source of cyber deterrence at the different levels of analysis. How might public and private attribution of an attack deter, coerce, or otherwise influence individual, non-state, and state cyber challengers?\n\nFourth, US cyber deterrence is not chiefly about military coercion. Notwithstanding Cybercom's emerging role in providing the US government with offensive and military options to punish cyber aggressors globally, US cyber deterrence strategy has emphasised the importance of several other departments and agencies, including DOJ, DHS, FBI, State Department and various others, for both denying and punishing cyberattacks. The goal is to equip the US government with as wide a set of coercive levers as possible, crossing domains as needed, to provide a truly whole of government approach to cybersecurity and cyber deterrence. The role of the DOJ (and the FBI by extension) is especially enlightening. By publicly indicting foreign nationals for cyberattacks conducted on American computer systems, the US government underscores the fact that contemporary cyber deterrence transcends traditional defence policy and military doctrine. By establishing the DOJ as a core pillar of its punishment strategy, the US signals its intention to use criminal justice tools to deter not just criminal attacks but attacks conducted by nation state adversaries. In doing so, the US further blurs the line between state institutions and individuals, by sanctioning the latter for actions they perpetrated while working for or on behalf of foreign governments, militaries, or intelligence agencies. For deterrence theory, the burgeoning role non-military and non-kinetic coercion have had in US cyber deterrence posture suggests that academics must redouble their efforts at multidisciplinarity. Academic silos still exist in the manner in which deterrence theory is developed and tested. To some degree, deterrence from within IR theory is privileged over that stemming from criminology, psychology, and terrorism and intelligence studies. This is especially the case when it comes to deterrence in national security and international affairs. Though the fourth wave of deterrence scholarship has gone to",
    "some length to bridge the disciplinary divide, more cross-fertilisation is needed among disciplines to better reflect and speak to developments taking place in practice. What theoretical lessons do non-IR approaches to deterrence hold for how IR theories of cyber deterrence are developed and improved?\n\nFifth, US cyber deterrence is not solely about states, either. Rather, non-state actors figure prominently in American strategic doctrine as both potential perpetrators of cyberattacks targeting US infrastructure, and, more importantly for our purposes, as potential partners that amplify US cyber deterrence. For instance, private sector and individual victims of cyberattack share information, voluntarily, about attacks with law enforcement and intelligence. This provides governments with a better understanding of the nature, prevalence and evolution of certain types of attack, which can lead to improvements in defence and denial. In other cases, as highlighted by Facebook, Microsoft, and Marcus Hutchins -- a private British citizen who noticed a ‘kill switch' within WannaCry's code and took action to temporarily shut the attack down -- non-state actors can track and impede attacks without government oversight, further denying perpetrators the fruits of their labour. In still other cases, non-state actors, including companies, individuals, academics, NGOs, and other such groups, can augment US cyber security and deterrence by denial efforts by practicing better cyber hygiene -- i.e. updating passwords or patching software. This functions to further deny challengers easy opportunities to conduct successful attacks. On this front, Washington is exploring how it might compel (or, even, demand) private sector partners, contractors, and suppliers to do more to defend and secure their own networks, data and assets, as a precondition for working with and on behalf of the US government. In February 2018, for instance, Deputy Defense Secretary Patrick Shanahan, in discussing the 2018 National Defense Strategy at a conference, warned that DoD wanted the cyber security ‘bar to be set so high, it will become the condition of doing business' with the Pentagon; for industry, cybersecurity will be ‘foundational to business with DoD'. The point, in this case, is to develop a whole of society deterrence posture, rather than simply a whole of government approach. The logic of all of this goes back to a statement made in President Bush's opening letter to the first 2003 cybersecurity strategy, oft repeated since: ‘Securing cyberspace is an extraordinarily difficult strategic challenge that requires a coordinated and focused effort from our entire society -- the federal government, state and local governments, the private sector, and the American people'. ‘Every American', the strategy continues ‘who can contribute to securing part of cyberspace is encouraged",
    "to do so. The federal government invites the creation of, and participation in, public-private partnerships to raise cybersecurity awareness, train personnel, stimulate market forces, improve technology, identify and remediate vulnerabilities, exchange information and plan recovery operations. Of the many ways in which the US government has previously approached deterrence in the other domains of warfare, this general call to arms is unique to cyberspace and cyber deterrence.\n\nIn response, scholars of deterrence need to explore the nature and feasibility of non-state cyber deterrence. Cyberspace is not a state-centric environment. On the contrary: states and militaries represent a tiny fraction of all the communities, individuals, and non-state actors that constitute cyberspace. Objects, too, have cyber presence. Projections suggest that 50 billion items -- from smart-homes to smart-cars, and from ‘intelligent' construction materials to wireless medical devices -- will be networked online as part of the IoT through an array of embedded electronics, software and sensors. All to say, the vast majority of interactions and the vast majority of the data created online is non-state in nature. Cyber deterrence theory must move beyond its narrow focus on states to account for the diversity of actors and objects that populate cyberspace. What is the equivalent in cyberspace of businesses, community groups, and homeowners employing storefront lighting, neighbourhood watch programs, and anti-burglary systems to deter physical attacks, theft and robbery? Can non-state actors be agents of their own deterrence in cyberspace?\n\n## Disclosure statement\n\nNo potential conflict of interest was reported by the author.\n\n## Notes on contributor\n\nAlex S. Wilner is an Assistant Professor of international affairs at the Norman Paterson School of International Affairs, Canada (https://alexwilner.com). His books and edited volumes include Deterring Terrorism: Theory and Practice (Stanford University Press, 2012) and Deterring Rational Fanatics (University of Pennsylvania Press, 2015).",

    "abstract": "When compared to advancements in conceptualising deterrence in other domains, cyber deterrence is still in it messy infancy. In some ways cyber deterrence practice outpaces cyber deterrence theory. Tactics, strategy, doctrine, and policy are developed and put to use even before corresponding theories are properly understood. This article analyses how American cyber deterrence has been implemented over the past two decades in order to inform ongoing debates within the academic study of deterrence, and to provide insights from practice for how cyber deterrence theory can be better conceived and refined. While President Donald Trump upbraided the previous administration's apparent weakness in cyberspace – ‘We’re run by people that don’t know what they’re doing’ – his administration has itself struggled to table a coherent cyber deterrence strategy, despite repeated promises to do so quickly.¹ As a presidential candidate, Trump promised to ‘develop the offensive cyber capabilities we need to deter attacks by both state and non-state actors’.² Two weeks following his November 2017 election, President-elect Trump went on to explain further – that he would build ‘a comprehensive plan to protect America’s vital infrastructure against cyberattacks’.³ And months later still, as President, he signed Executive Order (EO) 13800 in May 2017 – an updated draft of a January 2017 EO that had been scraped at the last minute – on Strengthening the Cybersecurity of Federal Networks and Critical Infrastructure. The EO describes a plan to reassess all elements of US cybersecurity across and beyond the government. In terms of cyber deterrence specifically, EO 13800 calls on various agencies and departments – including the Departments of State, Treasury, Commerce, and Homeland Security, along with the Director of CONTACT Alex S. Wilner alex.wilner@carleton.ca Norman Paterson School of International Affairs, Carleton University, Colonel By drive, Richcraft Hall, Ottawa, Canada ¹Joseph Marks, 'Trump Acknowledges Russian Hacking, points to Poor Defenses', NextGov, 11 January 2017. ²Donald J. Trump, 'Cybersecurity', Presidential Campaign Website, 2016, https://www.donaldjtrump.com/policies/cyber-security (accessed January 2017). ³Michael Shear and Julie Hirschfeld Davis, 'Trump, on YouTube, Pledges to Create Jobs', New York Times, 21 November 2016. © 2019 Informa UK Limited, trading as Taylor & Francis Group # A. S. WILNER National Intelligence – to jointly submit a report ‘on the Nation’s strategic options for deterring adversaries’ in cyberspace. $^{4}$ The report was to be submitted within 90 days (August 2017). A public version of the report eventually appeared in May 2018. $^{5}$ President Trump was also slow to appoint or approve several top-level federal cybersecurity and technology positions – including, notably, Federal Chief Information Security Officer, and the head of DHS's cybersecurity and infrastructure protection division. $^{6}$ These positions are critical to guiding US cybersecurity policy and planning, including in the area of cyber deterrence. Despite all of this, (former) White House Homeland Security advisor, Thomas Bossert – who helped craft cybersecurity policy under US President George W. Bush – revealed in October 2017, that the Trump Administration was determined to eventually publish a new cybersecurity strategy based on EO 13800. $^{7}$ And in his January 2017 Confirmation Hearing, General James Mattis, President Trump's (former) Secretary of Defense, suggested that a comprehensive 'cyber doctrine' might be ready by July 2018. $^{8}$ The new National Cyber Strategy was eventually published in September 2018, after an earlier public summary was held back in May 2018. $^{9}$ Although a US cyber deterrence strategy distinct from a broader cyber doctrine has yet to be established or fully leveraged, bits and pieces of the strategy exist, peppered about in different government departments and agencies. Indeed, US cyber deterrence has evolved a great deal since the mid-2000s. The purpose of this article is twofold. First, to better anticipate where US cyber deterrence is heading in practice by exploring the manner in which it has evolved through different presidential administrations. And second, to trace the evolution of US cyber deterrence to date to explore cyber deterrence in theory. When compared to theoretical advancements in conceptualising and implementing deterrence in other domains, including, $^{4}$The White House, Executive Order 13800, Strengthening the Cybersecurity of Federal Networks and Critical infrastructure, 11 May 2017. $^{5}$US Department of State, Office of the Coordinator for Cyber Issues, ‘Recommendations to the President on Deterring Adversaries and Better Protecting the American People from Cyber Threats’, 31 May 2018. $^{6}$Joseph Marks, ‘Many Top Cybersecurity Posts Remain Empty – and Not on Purpose’, DefenseOne, 10 November 2017; Mohana Ravindranath, ‘Many CFO Act federal Agencies are Still being Lead by Acting Chief Information Officers’, Nextgov, 17 November 2017. $^{7}$In a moment of further intrigue, Bossert resigned his post in April 2018, on the request of John Bolton, Trump’s newly-appointed national security advisor. Bossert’s departure was followed a week later by Rob Joyce’s, the White House cybersecurity coordinator. President Trump later eliminated Joyce’s position altogether, in May 2018. Shaun Waterman, ‘Bossert Promises New National Cybersecurity Strategy’, CyberScoop, 24 October 2017; Reuters, ‘Trump Scaps Cyber Czar Post after first Appointee Leaves: White House’, 15 May 2018; Ellen Nakashima, ‘White House Cybersecurity Official to Return to NSA’, The Washington Post, 16 April 2018. $^{8}$US Senate Confirmation Hearing, Advanced Policy Questions for James Mattis, 12 January 2017. $^{9}$Chris Bing, ‘National Security Council Delays Publication of Cyber Strategy over Inclusion of “Offensive” Measures’, CyberScoop, 15 May 2018; Sean Lyngaas, ‘White House Announces federal cyber strategy, vows to go on Offensive’, CyberScoop, 20 September 2018. for instance, in conventional and nuclear warfare, in space, and in counterterrorism, cyber deterrence theory is still in its messy infancy. By the standards of deterrence theory, which has a robust and cumulative pedigree dating back over a century, cyber deterrence is an innovative but imperfectly understood idea. The bulk of the academic literature on cyber deterrence was produced only very recently, over the past fifteen years or so. Moreover, in some important ways, US cyber deterrence practice outpaces cyber deterrence theory. Tactics, strategy, doctrine and policy have been developed, unveiled and put to use even before corresponding theories from academia are properly understood, tested and refined. If practice precedes theory, then a reflection of how this practice progressed over time may help inform theory more broadly. Combined, these circumstances leave scholars with a unique opportunity to evaluate an example of cyber deterrence practice in order to explain and better describe cyber deterrence theory. In methodological terms, this article explores how American cyber deterrence has been implemented in order to better highlight theory more broadly. The United States, by dint of its size, pre-eminence in cyberspace and decades of experience operationalising deterrence theory in other domains, represents a crucial case. Moreover, in empirical terms, the US government openly discusses cybersecurity, and has made various iterations of its cyber deterrence strategy and doctrine publicly available. While a comprehensive US cyber deterrence strategy has yet to be tabled, fragments of the strategy can be patched together, piecemeal, from various US government bodies. A record of thought and practice exists; wading through it provides insights for theory. The article begins with a summary of the academic literature on deterrence theory dating back to the beginning of the Cold War. The rationale here is to outline the nuts and bolts of deterrence theory, highlight conceptual prerequisites, illustrate the many ways deterrence has been operationalised, and briefly assess the ‘four waves' of deterrence scholarship. Next, the article takes a broad look at cyber deterrence theory in particular. The purpose is to discuss the core debates and research clusters embedded within this particular sub-field of the literature. Doing so will provide a theoretical foundation against which the empirical work can be paired against. An exploration of US cyber deterrence then follows. This section will provide a detailed and linear reflection of US cyber deterrence strategy, doctrine and policy stretching back to the early 2000s. The analysis will piece together how US strategy has unfolded and evolved over time, and help identify specific concepts, trends and assumptions baked into strategic # A. S. WILNER discourse. Highlights from the US example will then be analysed in the conclusion of the article. The way the United States has put cyber deterrence into practice over time provides lessons and insights for how cyber deterrence theory might be better conceived, approached and refined.",
